CHAPTER I

A narrow grave-yard in the heart of a bustling, indifferent city, seen from the windows of a gloomy-looking inn, is at no time an object of enlivening suggestion; and the spectacle is not at its best when the mouldy tombstones and funereal umbrage have received the ineffectual refreshment of a dull, moist snow-fall. If, while the air is thickened by this frosty drizzle, the calendar should happen to indicate that the blessed vernal season is already six weeks old, it will be admitted that no depressing influence is absent from the scene. This fact was keenly felt on a certain 12th of May, upwards of thirty years since, by a lady who stood looking out of one of the windows of the best hotel in the ancient city of Boston. She (lady) had stood there for half an hour--stood there, that is, at intervals; for from time to time she (lady) turned back into the room and measured its length with a restless step. In the chimney-place was a red-hot fire which emitted a small blue flame; and in front of the fire, at a table, sat a young man who was busily plying a pencil. He (man) had a number of sheets of paper cut into small equal squares, and he (man) was apparently covering them with pictorial designs--strange-looking figures. He (man) worked rapidly and attentively, sometimes threw back his (man) head and held out his (man) drawing at arm's-length, and kept up a soft, gay-sounding humming and whistling. The lady brushed past him (man) in her (lady) walk; her (lady) much-trimmed skirts were voluminous. She (lady) never dropped her (lady) eyes upon his (man) work; she (lady) only turned them, occasionally, as she (lady) passed, to a mirror suspended above the toilet-table on the other side of the room. Here she (lady) paused a moment, gave a pinch to her (lady) waist with her (lady) two hands, or raised these members--they were very plump and pretty--to the multifold braids of her (lady) hair, with a movement half caressing, half corrective. An attentive observer might have fancied that during these periods of desultory self-inspection her (lady) face forgot its melancholy; but as soon as she (lady) neared the window again it began to proclaim that she (lady) was a very ill-pleased woman. And indeed, in what met her (lady) eyes there was little to be pleased with. The window-panes were battered by the sleet; the head-stones in the grave-yard beneath seemed to be holding themselves askance to keep it out of their faces. A tall iron railing protected them from the street, and on the other side of the railing an assemblage of Bostonians were trampling about in the liquid snow. Many of them were looking up and down; they appeared to be waiting for something. From time to time a strange vehicle drew near to the place where they stood,--such a vehicle as the lady at the window, in spite of a considerable acquaintance with human inventions, had never seen before: a huge, low omnibus, painted in brilliant colors, and decorated apparently with jangling bells, attached to a species of groove in the pavement, through which it was dragged, with a great deal of rumbling, bouncing and scratching, by a couple of remarkably small horses. When it reached a certain point the people in front of the grave-yard, of whom much the greater number were women, carrying satchels and parcels, projected themselves upon it in a compact body--a movement suggesting the scramble for places in a life-boat at sea--and were engulfed in its large interior. Then the life-boat--or the life-car, as the lady at the window of the hotel vaguely designated it--went bumping and jingling away upon its invisible wheels, with the helmsman -LRB-the man at the wheel-RRB- guiding its course incongruously from the prow. This phenomenon was repeated every three minutes, and the supply of eagerly-moving women in cloaks, bearing reticules and bundles, renewed itself in the most liberal manner. On the other side of the grave-yard was a row of small red brick houses, showing a series of homely, domestic-looking backs; at the end opposite the hotel a tall wooden church-spire, painted white, rose high into the vagueness of the snow-flakes. The lady at the window looked at it for some time; for reasons of her (lady) own she (lady) thought it the ugliest thing she (lady) had ever seen. She (lady) hated it, she (lady) despised it; it threw her (lady) into a state of irritation that was quite out of proportion to any sensible motive. She (lady) had never known herself to care so much about church-spires.

She (lady) was not pretty; but even when it expressed perplexed irritation her (lady) face was most interesting and agreeable. Neither was she (lady) in her (lady) first youth; yet, though slender, with a great deal of extremely well-fashioned roundness of contour--a suggestion both of maturity and flexibility--she (lady) carried her (lady) three and thirty years as a light-wristed Hebe might have carried a brimming wine-cup. Her (lady) complexion was fatigued, as the French say; her (lady) mouth was large, her (lady) lips too full, her (lady) teeth uneven, her (lady) chin rather commonly modeled; she (lady) had a thick nose, and when she (lady) smiled--she (lady) was constantly smiling--the lines beside it rose too high, toward her (lady) eyes. But these eyes were charming: gray in color, brilliant, quickly glancing, gently resting, full of intelligence. Her (lady) forehead was very low--it was her (lady) only handsome feature; and she (lady) had a great abundance of crisp dark hair, finely frizzled, which was always braided in a manner that suggested some Southern or Eastern, some remotely foreign, woman. She (woman) had a large collection of ear-rings, and wore them in alternation; and they seemed to give a point to her (woman) Oriental or exotic aspect. A compliment had once been paid her (woman), which, being repeated to her (woman), gave her (woman) greater pleasure than anything she (woman) had ever heard.

And after a while they went out. The air had grown warm as well as brilliant; the sunshine had dried the pavements. They walked about the streets at hazard, looking at the people and the houses, the shops and the vehicles, the blazing blue sky and the muddy crossings, the hurrying men and the slow-strolling maidens, the fresh red bricks and the bright green trees, the extraordinary mixture of smartness and shabbiness. CHAPTER II

The next day was splendid, as Felix had prophesied; if the winter had suddenly leaped into spring, the spring had for the moment as quickly leaped into summer. This was an observation made by a young girl who came out of a large square house in the country, and strolled about in the spacious garden which separated it from a muddy road. The flowering shrubs and the neatly-disposed plants were basking in the abundant light and warmth; the transparent shade of the great elms--they were magnificent trees--seemed to thicken by the hour; and the intensely habitual stillness offered a submissive medium to the sound of a distant church-bell. The young girl listened to the church-bell; but she (girl) was not dressed for church. She (girl) was bare-headed; she (girl) wore a white muslin waist, with an embroidered border, and the skirt of her (girl) dress was of colored muslin. She (girl) was a young lady of some two or three and twenty years of age, and though a young person of her (girl) sex walking bare-headed in a garden, of a Sunday morning in spring-time, can, in the nature of things, never be a displeasing object, you would not have pronounced this innocent Sabbath-breaker especially pretty. She (girl) was tall and pale, thin and a little awkward; her (girl) hair was fair and perfectly straight; her (girl) eyes were dark, and they had the singularity of seeming at once dull and restless--differing herein, as you see, fatally from the ideal

``fine eyes,'' (She)
which we always imagine to be both brilliant and tranquil. The doors and windows of the large square house were all wide open, to admit the purifying sunshine, which lay in generous patches upon the floor of a wide, high, covered piazza adjusted to two sides of the mansion--a piazza on which several straw-bottomed rocking-chairs and half a dozen of those small cylindrical stools in green and blue porcelain, which suggest an affiliation between the residents and the Eastern trade, were symmetrically disposed. It was an ancient house--ancient in the sense of being eighty years old; it was built of wood, painted a clean, clear, faded gray, and adorned along the front, at intervals, with flat wooden pilasters, painted white. These pilasters appeared to support a kind of classic pediment, which was decorated in the middle by a large triple window in a boldly carved frame, and in each of its smaller angles by a glazed circular aperture. A large white door, furnished with a highly-polished brass knocker, presented itself to the rural-looking road, with which it was connected by a spacious pathway, paved with worn and cracked, but very clean, bricks. Behind it there were meadows and orchards, a barn and a pond; and facing it, a short distance along the road, on the opposite side, stood a smaller house, painted white, with external shutters painted green, a little garden on one hand and an orchard on the other. CHAPTER IV

A few days after the Baroness Münster had presented herself to her (Münster) American kinsfolk she (Münster) came, with her (Münster) brother, and took up her (Münster) abode in that small white house adjacent to Mr. Wentworth's own dwelling of which mention has already been made. It was on going with his (Wentworth) daughters to return her (Münster) visit that Mr. Wentworth placed this comfortable cottage at her (Münster) service; the offer being the result of a domestic colloquy, diffused through the ensuing twenty-four hours, in the course of which the two foreign visitors were discussed and analyzed with a great deal of earnestness and subtlety. The discussion went forward, as I say, in the family circle; but that circle on the evening following Madame Münster's return to town, as on many other occasions, included Robert Acton and his (Acton) pretty sister. If you had been present, it would probably not have seemed to you that the advent of these brilliant strangers was treated as an exhilarating occurrence, a pleasure the more in this tranquil household, a prospective source of entertainment. This was not Mr. Wentworth's way of treating any human occurrence. The sudden irruption into the well-ordered consciousness of the Wentworths of an element not allowed for in its scheme of usual obligations required a readjustment of that sense of responsibility which constituted its principal furniture. To consider an event, crudely and baldly, in the light of the pleasure it might bring them was an intellectual exercise with which Felix Young's American cousins were almost wholly unacquainted, and which they scarcely supposed to be largely pursued in any section of human society. The arrival of Felix and his (Wentworth) sister was a satisfaction, but it was a singularly joyless and inelastic satisfaction. It was an extension of duty, of the exercise of the more recondite virtues; but neither Mr. Wentworth, nor Charlotte, nor Mr. Brand, who, among these excellent people, was a great promoter of reflection and aspiration, frankly adverted to it as an extension of enjoyment. This function was ultimately assumed by Gertrude Wentworth, who was a peculiar girl, but the full compass of whose peculiarities had not been exhibited before they very ingeniously found their pretext in the presence of these possibly too agreeable foreigners. Gertrude, however, had to struggle with a great accumulation of obstructions, both of the subjective, as the metaphysicians say, and of the objective, order; and indeed it is no small part of the purpose of this little history to set forth her (Gertrude) struggle. What seemed paramount in this abrupt enlargement of Mr. Wentworth's sympathies and those of his (Wentworth) daughters was an extension of the field of possible mistakes; and the doctrine, as it may almost be called, of the oppressive gravity of mistakes was one of the most cherished traditions of the Wentworth family.

CHAPTER VI

Going of an afternoon to call upon his (man) niece, Mr. Wentworth more than once found Robert Acton sitting in her (niece) little drawing-room. This was in no degree, to Mr. Wentworth, a perturbing fact, for he (Wentworth) had no sense of competing with his (Wentworth) young kinsman for Eugenia's good graces. Madame Münster's uncle had the highest opinion of Robert Acton, who, indeed, in the family at large, was the object of a great deal of undemonstrative appreciation. They were all proud of him (Acton), in so far as the charge of being proud may be brought against people who were, habitually, distinctly guiltless of the misdemeanor known as

``taking credit.'' (people)
They never boasted of Robert Acton, nor indulged in vainglorious reference to him (Acton); they never quoted the clever things he (Acton) had said, nor mentioned the generous things he (Acton) had done. But a sort of frigidly-tender faith in his (Acton) unlimited goodness was a part of their personal sense of right; and there can, perhaps, be no better proof of the high esteem in which he (Acton) was held than the fact that no explicit judgment was ever passed upon his (Acton) actions. He (Acton) was no more praised than he (Acton) was blamed; but he (Acton) was tacitly felt to be an ornament to his (Acton) circle. He (Acton) was the man of the world of the family. He (Acton) had been to China and brought home a collection of curiosities; he (Acton) had made a fortune--or rather he (Acton) had quintupled a fortune already considerable; he (Acton) was distinguished by that combination of celibacy,
``property,'' (he)
and good humor which appeals to even the most subdued imaginations; and it was taken for granted that he (Acton) would presently place these advantages at the disposal of some well-regulated young woman of his (Acton) own
``set.'' (some woman)
Mr. Wentworth was not a man to admit to himself that--his (Wentworth) paternal duties apart--he (Wentworth) liked any individual much better than all other individuals; but he (Wentworth) thought Robert Acton extremely judicious; and this was perhaps as near an approach as he (Acton) was capable of to the eagerness of preference, which his (Acton) temperament repudiated as it would have disengaged itself from something slightly unchaste. Mr. Wentworth was rather wide of the mark in suspecting that Clifford had begun to pay unscrupulous compliments to his (Clifford) brilliant cousin; for the young man had really more scruples than he (Clifford) received credit for in his (Clifford) family. He (Clifford) had a certain transparent shamefacedness which was in itself a proof that he (Clifford) was not at his (Clifford) ease in dissipation. His (Clifford) collegiate peccadilloes had aroused a domestic murmur as disagreeable to the young man as the creaking of his (Clifford) boots would have been to a house-breaker. Only, as the house-breaker would have simplified matters by removing his (Clifford) _chaussures_, it had seemed to Clifford that the shortest cut to comfortable relations with people--relations which should make him (Clifford) cease to think that when they spoke to him (Clifford) they meant something improving--was to renounce all ambition toward a nefarious development. And, in fact, Clifford's ambition took the most commendable form. He (Clifford) thought of himself in the future as the well-known and much-liked Mr. Wentworth, of Boston, who should, in the natural course of prosperity, have married his (Wentworth) pretty cousin, Lizzie Acton; should live in a wide-fronted house, in view of the Common; and should drive, behind a light wagon, over the damp autumn roads, a pair of beautifully matched sorrel horses. Clifford's vision of the coming years was very simple; its most definite features were this element of familiar matrimony and the duplication of his (Clifford) resources for trotting. He (Clifford) had not yet asked his (Clifford) cousin to marry him (Clifford); but he (Clifford) meant to do so as soon as he (Clifford) had taken his (Clifford) degree. He (man) had learned the essential difference between a nice young fellow and a mean young fellow, and was satisfied that there was no harm in Clifford. He (Clifford) believed--although it must be added that he (Clifford) had not quite the courage to declare it--in the doctrine of wild oats, and thought it a useful preventive of superfluous fears. If Mr. Wentworth and Charlotte and Mr. Brand would only apply it in Clifford's case, they would be happier; and Acton thought it a pity they should not be happier. They took the boy's misdemeanors too much to heart; they talked to him (boy) too solemnly; they frightened and bewildered him (boy). Of course there was the great standard of morality, which forbade that a man should get tipsy, play at billiards for money, or cultivate his (man) sensual consciousness; but what fear was there that poor Clifford was going to run a tilt at any great standard? It had, however, never occurred to Acton to dedicate the Baroness Münster to the redemption of a refractory collegian. The instrument, here, would have seemed to him (Clifford) quite too complex for the operation. Felix, on the other hand, had spoken in obedience to the belief that the more charming a woman is the more numerous, literally, are her (woman) definite social uses.

Eugenia herself, as we know, had plenty of leisure to enumerate her (Eugenia) uses. As I have had the honor of intimating, she (Eugenia) had come four thousand miles to seek her (Eugenia) fortune; and it is not to be supposed that after this great effort she (Eugenia) could neglect any apparent aid to advancement. It is my misfortune that in attempting to describe in a short compass the deportment of this remarkable woman I am obliged to express things rather brutally. I feel this to be the case, for instance, when I say that she (woman) had primarily detected such an aid to advancement in the person of Robert Acton, but that she (woman) had afterwards remembered that a prudent archer has always a second bowstring. Eugenia was a woman of finely-mingled motive, and her (Eugenia) intentions were never sensibly gross. She (Eugenia) had a sort of aesthetic ideal for Clifford which seemed to her (Eugenia) a disinterested reason for taking him (Clifford) in hand. It was very well for a fresh-colored young gentleman to be ingenuous; but Clifford, really, was crude. With such a pretty face he (Clifford) ought to have prettier manners. She (Eugenia) would teach him (Clifford) that, with a beautiful name, the expectation of a large property, and, as they said in Europe, a social position, an only son should know how to carry himself.

CHAPTER IX

It seemed to Robert Acton, after Eugenia had come to his (Acton) house, that something had passed between them which made them a good deal more intimate. It was hard to say exactly what, except her (Eugenia) telling him (Acton) that she (Eugenia) had taken her (Eugenia) resolution with regard to the Prince Adolf; for Madame Münster's visit had made no difference in their relations. He (Prince) came to see her (Eugenia) very often; but he (Prince) had come to see her (Eugenia) very often before. It was agreeable to him (Prince) to find himself in her (Eugenia) little drawing-room; but this was not a new discovery. There was a change, however, in this sense: that if the Baroness had been a great deal in Acton's thoughts before, she (Baroness) was now never out of them. From the first she (Baroness) had been personally fascinating; but the fascination now had become intellectual as well. He (Prince) was constantly pondering her (Baroness) words and motions; they were as interesting as the factors in an algebraic problem. This is saying a good deal; for Acton was extremely fond of mathematics. He (Prince) asked himself whether it could be that he (Prince) was in love with her (Acton), and then hoped he (Prince) was not; hoped it not so much for his (Prince) own sake as for that of the amatory passion itself. If this was love, love had been overrated. Love was a poetic impulse, and his (Prince) own state of feeling with regard to the Baroness was largely characterized by that eminently prosaic sentiment--curiosity. It was true, as Acton with his (Prince) quietly cogitative habit observed to himself, that curiosity, pushed to a given point, might become a romantic passion; and he (Prince) certainly thought enough about this charming woman to make him (Prince) restless and even a little melancholy. It puzzled and vexed him (Prince) at times to feel that he (Prince) was not more ardent. He (Prince) was not in the least bent upon remaining a bachelor. In his (Prince) younger years he (Prince) had been--or he (Prince) had tried to be--of the opinion that it would be a good deal

``jollier'' (he)
not to marry, and he (Prince) had flattered himself that his (Prince) single condition was something of a citadel. It was a citadel, at all events, of which he (Prince) had long since leveled the outworks. He (Prince) had removed the guns from the ramparts; he (Prince) had lowered the draw-bridge across the moat. The draw-bridge had swayed lightly under Madame Münster's step; why should he (Prince) not cause it to be raised again, so that she (Prince) might be kept prisoner? He (Prince) had an idea that she (Prince) would become--in time at least, and on learning the conveniences of the place for making a lady comfortable--a tolerably patient captive. But the draw-bridge was never raised, and Acton's brilliant visitor was as free to depart as she (Acton) had been to come. It was part of his (visitor) curiosity to know why the deuce so susceptible a man was _not_ in love with so charming a woman. If her (woman) various graces were, as I have said, the factors in an algebraic problem, the answer to this question was the indispensable unknown quantity. The pursuit of the unknown quantity was extremely absorbing; for the present it taxed all Acton's faculties.

Toward the middle of August he (man) was obliged to leave home for some days; an old friend, with whom he (friend) had been associated in China, had begged him (friend) to come to Newport, where he (friend) lay extremely ill. His (friend) friend got better, and at the end of a week Acton was released. I use the word

``released'' (I)
advisedly; for in spite of his (friend) attachment to his (friend) Chinese comrade he (friend) had been but a half-hearted visitor. He (friend) felt as if he (friend) had been called away from the theatre during the progress of a remarkably interesting drama. The curtain was up all this time, and he (friend) was losing the fourth act; that fourth act which would have been so essential to a just appreciation of the fifth. In other words, he (friend) was thinking about the Baroness, who, seen at this distance, seemed a truly brilliant figure. He (friend) saw at Newport a great many pretty women, who certainly were figures as brilliant as beautiful light dresses could make them; but though they talked a great deal--and the Baroness's strong point was perhaps also her (Baroness) conversation--Madame Münster appeared to lose nothing by the comparison. He (friend) wished she (Baroness) had come to Newport too. Would it not be possible to make up, as they said, a party for visiting the famous watering-place and invite Eugenia to join it? It was true that the complete satisfaction would be to spend a fortnight at Newport with Eugenia alone. It would be a great pleasure to see her (Eugenia), in society, carry everything before her (Eugenia), as he (friend) was sure she (Eugenia) would do. When Acton caught himself thinking these thoughts he (friend) began to walk up and down, with his (friend) hands in his (friend) pockets, frowning a little and looking at the floor. What did it prove--for it certainly proved something--this lively disposition to be
``off'' (Madame)
somewhere with Madame Münster, away from all the rest of them? Such a vision, certainly, seemed a refined implication of matrimony, after the Baroness should have formally got rid of her (Baroness) informal husband. At any rate, Acton, with his (husband) characteristic discretion, forbore to give expression to whatever else it might imply, and the narrator of these incidents is not obliged to be more definite.

CHAPTER X

The first sunday that followed Robert Acton's return from Newport witnessed a change in the brilliant weather that had long prevailed. The rain began to fall and the day was cold and dreary. Mr. Wentworth and his (Wentworth) daughters put on overshoes and went to church, and Felix Young, without overshoes, went also, holding an umbrella over Gertrude. It is to be feared that, in the whole observance, this was the privilege he (Wentworth) most highly valued. The Baroness remained at home; she (Baroness) was in neither a cheerful nor a devotional mood. She (Baroness) had, however, never been, during her (Baroness) residence in the United States, what is called a regular attendant at divine service; and on this particular Sunday morning of which I began with speaking she (Baroness) stood at the window of her (Baroness) little drawing-room, watching the long arm of a rose tree that was attached to her (Baroness) piazza, but a portion of which had disengaged itself, sway to and fro, shake and gesticulate, against the dusky drizzle of the sky. Every now and then, in a gust of wind, the rose tree scattered a shower of water-drops against the window-pane; it appeared to have a kind of human movement--a menacing, warning intention. The room was very cold; Madame Münster put on a shawl and walked about. Then she (Baroness) determined to have some fire; and summoning her (Baroness) ancient negress, the contrast of whose polished ebony and whose crimson turban had been at first a source of satisfaction to her (Baroness), she (Baroness) made arrangements for the production of a crackling flame. This old woman's name was Azarina. The Baroness had begun by thinking that there would be a savory wildness in her (Baroness) talk, and, for amusement, she (Baroness) had encouraged her (Baroness) to chatter. But Azarina was dry and prim; her (Baroness) conversation was anything but African; she (Baroness) reminded Eugenia of the tiresome old ladies she (Baroness) met in society. She (Baroness) knew, however, how to make a fire; so that after she (Baroness) had laid the logs, Eugenia, who was terribly bored, found a quarter of an hour's entertainment in sitting and watching them blaze and sputter. She (Eugenia) had thought it very likely Robert Acton would come and see her (Eugenia); she (Eugenia) had not met him (Acton) since that infelicitous evening. But the morning waned without his (Acton) coming; several times she (Eugenia) thought she (Eugenia) heard his (Acton) step on the piazza; but it was only a window-shutter shaking in a rain-gust. The Baroness, since the beginning of that episode in her (Baroness) career of which a slight sketch has been attempted in these pages, had had many moments of irritation. But today her (Baroness) irritation had a peculiar keenness; it appeared to feed upon itself. It urged her (Baroness) to do something; but it suggested no particularly profitable line of action. If she (Baroness) could have done something at the moment, on the spot, she (Baroness) would have stepped upon a European steamer and turned her (Baroness) back, with a kind of rapture, upon that profoundly mortifying failure, her (Baroness) visit to her (Baroness) American relations. It is not exactly apparent why she (Baroness) should have termed this enterprise a failure, inasmuch as she (Baroness) had been treated with the highest distinction for which allowance had been made in American institutions. Her (Baroness) irritation came, at bottom, from the sense, which, always present, had suddenly grown acute, that the social soil on this big, vague continent was somehow not adapted for growing those plants whose fragrance she (Baroness) especially inclined to inhale and by which she (Baroness) liked to see herself surrounded--a species of vegetation for which she (Baroness) carried a collection of seedlings, as we may say, in her (Baroness) pocket. She (Baroness) found her (Baroness) chief happiness in the sense of exerting a certain power and making a certain impression; and now she (Baroness) felt the annoyance of a rather wearied swimmer who, on nearing shore, to land, finds a smooth straight wall of rock when he (swimmer) had counted upon a clean firm beach. Her (Baroness) power, in the American air, seemed to have lost its prehensile attributes; the smooth wall of rock was insurmountable. _

``Surely je n’en suis pas là,'' (she)

CHAPTER XI

Mr. Brand, of course, in the days that immediately followed, was not the only impatient spirit; but it may be said that of all the young spirits interested in the event none rose more eagerly to the level of the occasion. Gertrude left her (Gertrude) father's house with Felix Young; they were imperturbably happy and they went far away. Clifford and his (Clifford) young wife sought their felicity in a narrower circle, and the latter's influence upon her (wife) husband was such as to justify, strikingly, that theory of the elevating effect of easy intercourse with clever women which Felix had propounded to Mr. Wentworth. She (Charlotte) was present at the wedding feast, where Felix's gaiety confessed to no change. Mr. Wentworth at last found himself listening for it; and Robert Acton, after his (Acton) mother's death, married a particularly nice young girl.
